</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height: 1.35em;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.global.toshiba/ww/top.html">global.toshiba</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below" style="line-height: 1.35em;"><b>Footnotes&#160;/&#32;references</b><br /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409"><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><sup>a.</sup> Fiscal Year 2021 (FY2021) in this article is from 1 April 2021 to 31 March 2022.</li><li><sup>b.</sup> Foundation date for one of the predecessor companies. The current company was established in 1904 as the direct successor of said company and its legal successor was founded in 1939.</li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Toshiba Corporation</b><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">株式会社東芝</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kabushikigaisha Tōshiba</i></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1177148991">.mw-parser-output .IPA-label-small{font-size:85%}.mw-parser-output .references .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .infobox .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .navbox .IPA-label-small{font-size:100%}</style><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">English: </span><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ʃ/: &#39;sh&#39; in &#39;shy&#39;">ʃ</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/ɒ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">ɒ</span></span>-,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span></span>-/</a></span></span><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>)</span> is a Japanese <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational</a> electronics company headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Minato,_Tokyo" title="Minato, Tokyo">Minato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. Its diversified products and services include power, industrial and social infrastructure systems, elevators and escalators, electronic components, <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk_drive" title="Hard disk drive">hard disk drives</a> (HDD), printers, batteries, lighting, as well as <a href="/wiki/Information_technology" title="Information technology">IT</a> solutions such as <a href="/wiki/Quantum_cryptography" title="Quantum cryptography">quantum cryptography</a> which has been in development at Cambridge Research Laboratory, Toshiba Europe, located in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, now being commercialised.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-quantumToshiba_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quantumToshiba-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It was one of the biggest manufacturers of <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Consumer_electronics" title="Consumer electronics">consumer electronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Home_appliance" title="Home appliance">home appliances</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medical_equipment" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical equipment">medical equipment</a>. As a semiconductor company and the inventor of <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">flash memory</a>, Toshiba had been one of the top 10 in the chip industry until its flash memory unit was spun off as Toshiba Memory, later <a href="/wiki/Kioxia" title="Kioxia">Kioxia</a>, in the late 2010s.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Toshiba name is derived from its former name, <b>Tokyo Shibaura Denki K.K.</b> (<b>Tokyo Shibaura Electric Co., Ltd</b>) which in turn was a 1939 merger between <a href="/wiki/Shibaura_Seisakusho" title="Shibaura Seisakusho">Shibaura Seisaku-sho</a> (founded in 1875) and <a href="/wiki/Hakunetsusha" title="Hakunetsusha">Tokyo Denki</a> (founded in 1890). The company name was officially changed to Toshiba Corporation in 1978. It was listed on the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Stock_Exchange" title="Tokyo Stock Exchange">Tokyo Stock Exchange</a> from 1949 to 2023 where it was a constituent of the <a href="/wiki/Nikkei_225" title="Nikkei 225">Nikkei 225</a> and <a href="/wiki/TOPIX" title="TOPIX">TOPIX</a> 100 indices (leaving both in August 2018, but returned to the latter in 2021), and the <a href="/wiki/Nagoya_Stock_Exchange" title="Nagoya Stock Exchange">Nagoya Stock Exchange</a>.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Technology_company" title="Technology company">technology company</a> with a long history and sprawling businesses, Toshiba is a household name in Japan and has long been viewed as a symbol of the country's technological prowess. Its reputation has since been affected following an accounting scandal in 2015 and the bankruptcy of subsidiary energy company <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Company" title="Westinghouse Electric Company">Westinghouse</a> in 2017, after which it was forced to shed a number of underperforming businesses, essentially eliminating the company's century-long presence in consumer markets.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-20170411_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20170411-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Toshiba announced on 12 November 2021 that it would split into three separate companies, respectively focusing on infrastructure, electronic devices, and all other remaining assets; the latter would retain the Toshiba name. It expected to complete the plan by March 2024,<sup id="cite_ref-Breakup_-_WSJ_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Breakup_-_WSJ-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> but the plan was challenged by stockholders, and at an extraordinary general meeting on 24 March 2022, they rejected the plan. They also rejected an alternative plan put forward by a large institutional investor that would have had the company search for buyers among private equity firms.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2023, however, the company announced it had accepted a <span style="white-space: nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Japanese_yen" title="Japanese yen">¥</a>2</span><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>trillion (<span style="white-space: nowrap">$15</span><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion) buyout offer from a consortium led by <a href="/wiki/Japan_Industrial_Partners" title="Japan Industrial Partners">Japan Industrial Partners</a> (JIP), a Tokyo-based private equity firm.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> On September 27, after the public offering was completed in the middle of that month, it was reported that it would be transferred to a new parent company, TBJH.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On December 22, 2023, it was announced that JIP's purchase of the company had been completed.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> This occurs two days after being delisted.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tanaka_Seisakusho">Tanaka Seisakusho</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Tanaka Seisakusho"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Tanaka Seisakusho</b><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">田中製作所</span></span>, Tanaka Engineering Works)</span> was the first company established by <a href="/wiki/Tanaka_Hisashige" title="Tanaka Hisashige">Tanaka Hisashige</a> (1799–1881), one of the most original and productive inventor-engineers during the Tokugawa / <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a>. Established on  11 July 1875,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> it was the first Japanese company to manufacture <a href="/wiki/Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a> equipment. It also manufactured switches, and miscellaneous electrical and communications equipment.
The company was inherited by Tanaka's adopted son, and later became half of the present Toshiba company. Several people who worked at Tanaka Seisakusho or who received Tanaka's guidance at a Kubusho (Ministry of Industries) factory later became pioneers themselves. These included <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miyoshi_Sh%C5%8Dichi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Miyoshi Shōichi (page does not exist)">Miyoshi Shōichi</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E4%B8%89%E5%90%89%E6%AD%A3%E4%B8%80" class="extiw" title="jp:三吉正一">jp</a>&#93;</span> who helped <a href="/w/index.php?title=Fujioka_Ichisuke&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Fujioka Ichisuke (page does not exist)">Fujioka Ichisuke</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E8%97%A4%E5%B2%A1%E5%B8%82%E5%8A%A9" class="extiw" title="jp:藤岡市助">jp</a>&#93;</span> make the first power generator in Japan and to establish a company, <a href="/wiki/Hakunetsusha" title="Hakunetsusha">Hakunetsusha</a> to make bulbs; <a href="/wiki/Oki_Kibatar%C5%8D" title="Oki Kibatarō">Oki Kibatarō</a>, the founder of the present Oki Denki (<a href="/wiki/Oki_Electric_Industry" title="Oki Electric Industry">Oki Electric Industry</a>); and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ishiguro_Keizabur%C5%8D&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ishiguro Keizaburō (page does not exist)">Ishiguro Keizaburō</a>, a co-founder of the present <a href="/wiki/Anritsu" title="Anritsu">Anritsu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Odagiri-Goto_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Odagiri-Goto-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the demise of the founder in 1881 Tanaka Seisakusho became partly owned by <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> and expanded into the production of <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedoes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">mines</a> at the request of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">Imperial Japanese Navy</a>, to become one of the largest manufacturing companies of the time. However, as the Navy started to use competitive bids and then build its own works, the demand decreased substantially and the company started to lose money. The main creditor to the company, <a href="/wiki/Mitsui" title="Mitsui">Mitsui</a> Bank, took over the insolvent company in 1893 and renamed it <a href="/wiki/Shibaura_Seisakusho" title="Shibaura Seisakusho">Shibaura Seisakusho</a> (Shibaura Engineering Works).<sup id="cite_ref-Odagiri-Goto_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Odagiri-Goto-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shibaura_Seisakusho">Shibaura Seisakusho</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Shibaura Seisakusho"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Shibaura Seisakusho</b><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">芝浦製作所</span></span>, Shibaura Engineering Works)</span> was the new name given to Tanaka Seisakusho after it was declared insolvent in 1893 and taken over by <a href="/wiki/Mitsui" title="Mitsui">Mitsui</a> Bank.
</p><p>In 1910, it formed a tie-up with <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> (GE), which, in exchange for technology, acquired about a quarter of the shares of Shibaura. The relation with GE continued until the beginning of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and resumed in 1953 with GE's 24 percent shareholding in the successor company, Tokyo Shibaura Denki. This percentage decreased substantially since then.<sup id="cite_ref-Odagiri-Goto_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Odagiri-Goto-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Hakunetsusha_.28Tokyo_Denki.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hakunetsusha_(Tokyo_Denki)">Hakunetsusha (Tokyo Denki)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hakunetsusha (Tokyo Denki)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Hakunetsusha</b><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">白熱舎</span></span>)</span> was a company established by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miyoshi_Sh%C5%8Dichi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Miyoshi Shōichi (page does not exist)">Miyoshi Shōichi</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Fujioka_Ichisuke&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Fujioka Ichisuke (page does not exist)">Fujioka Ichisuke</a>, two of Japan's industrial pioneers during the Tokugawa / <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a>. It specialized in the manufacture of light bulbs.
</p><p>The company was established in 1890 and started out by selling bulbs using bamboo filaments. However, following the opening up of trade with the West through the <a href="/wiki/Unequal_treaty" title="Unequal treaty">Unequal treaty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hakunetsusha" title="Hakunetsusha">Hakunetsusha</a> met with fierce competition from imports. Its bulb cost about 60 percent more than the imports and the quality was poorer. The company managed to survive with the booms after the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a> of 1894–95 and the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a> of 1904–05, but afterward its financial position was precarious. 
</p><p>In 1905 the company was renamed <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Denki" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokyo Denki">Tokyo Denki</a> (Tokyo Electric) and entered into a financial and technological collaboration with <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> of the US. General Electric acquired 51 percent share of ownership, sent a vice president, and provided the technology for bulb-making. Production equipment was bought from GE and Tokyo Denki soon started selling its products with GE's trademark.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1939_to_2000">1939 to 2000</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1939 to 2000"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Toshiba_Vacuum_tube_Radio.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Toshiba_Vacuum_tube_Radio.jpg/220px-Toshiba_Vacuum_tube_Radio.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Toshiba_Vacuum_tube_Radio.jpg/330px-Toshiba_Vacuum_tube_Radio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Toshiba_Vacuum_tube_Radio.jpg/440px-Toshiba_Vacuum_tube_Radio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1379" /></a><figcaption>AM-only Toshiba vacuum tube radio (1955)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Toshiba was founded in 1939 by the merger of Shibaura Seisakusho<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> and Tokyo Denki. The merger of Shibaura and Tokyo Denki created a new company called Tokyo Shibaura Denki (Tokyo Shibaura Electric) (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja-Hani"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9D%B1%E4%BA%AC" class="extiw" title="wikt:東京"><b>東</b>京</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%8A%9D%E6%B5%A6" class="extiw" title="wikt:芝浦"><b>芝</b>浦</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%9B%BB%E6%B0%97" class="extiw" title="wikt:電気">電気</a></span></span>). It was soon nicknamed Toshiba, but it was not until 1978 that the company was officially renamed Toshiba Corporation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The company was listed on the Tokyo Stock Exchange in May 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Expo85_toshiba.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Expo85_toshiba.jpg/220px-Expo85_toshiba.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Expo85_toshiba.jpg/330px-Expo85_toshiba.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Expo85_toshiba.jpg/440px-Expo85_toshiba.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="411" /></a><figcaption>The Toshiba pavilion at <a href="/wiki/Expo_%2785" class="mw-redirect" title="Expo &#39;85">Expo '85</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The group expanded rapidly, driven by a combination of organic growth and by acquisitions, buying heavy engineering, and primary industry firms in the 1940s and 1950s. Groups created include <a href="/wiki/EMI_Music_Japan" title="EMI Music Japan">Toshiba Music Industries/Toshiba EMI</a> (1960), Toshiba International Corporation (the 1970s), Toshiba Electrical Equipment (1974), Toshiba Chemical (1974), Toshiba Lighting and Technology (1989), Toshiba America Information Systems (1989) and Toshiba Carrier Corporation (1999).
</p><p>The first mini-split ductless <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioner" class="mw-redirect" title="Air conditioner">air conditioner</a> was sold in 1961 by Toshiba in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Toshiba is responsible for a number of Japanese firsts, including radar (1912)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="1912 is earlier than both Toshiba and Japan claim to have developed radar, should this be 1942? (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, the TAC digital computer (1954), transistor television, color CRTs<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and microwave oven (1959), <a href="/wiki/Videophone" class="mw-redirect" title="Videophone">color video phone</a> (1971), Japanese <a href="/wiki/Word_processor" title="Word processor">word processor</a> (1978), MRI system (1982), personal computer <a href="/wiki/Toshiba_Pasopia" title="Toshiba Pasopia">Pasopia</a> (1981), laptop personal computer (1986), NAND EEPROM (1991), DVD (1995), the <a href="/wiki/Libretto_(notebook)" class="mw-redirect" title="Libretto (notebook)">Libretto</a> sub-notebook personal computer (1996) and <a href="/wiki/HD_DVD" title="HD DVD">HD DVD</a> (2005).
</p><p>In 1977, Toshiba acquired the Brazilian company Semp (Sociedade Eletromercantil Paulista), subsequently forming Semp Toshiba through the combination of the two companies' South American operations.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:TOSHIBA_Logo_1950.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="In 1950, Tokyo Shibaura Denki was renamed Toshiba. This logo was used from 1950 to 1969." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/TOSHIBA_Logo_1950.svg/220px-TOSHIBA_Logo_1950.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="75" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/TOSHIBA_Logo_1950.svg/330px-TOSHIBA_Logo_1950.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/TOSHIBA_Logo_1950.svg/440px-TOSHIBA_Logo_1950.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1175" data-file-height="399" /></a><figcaption>In 1950, Tokyo Shibaura Denki was renamed Toshiba. This logo, known as the "Umbrella Mark", was used from 1950 to 1969, and then as a primary logo between 1969 and 1984. It was also used later on for hard drives.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure> <figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:TOSHIBA_Logo_1969.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/TOSHIBA_Logo_1969.svg/220px-TOSHIBA_Logo_1969.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="24" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/TOSHIBA_Logo_1969.svg/330px-TOSHIBA_Logo_1969.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/TOSHIBA_Logo_1969.svg/440px-TOSHIBA_Logo_1969.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="938" data-file-height="101" /></a><figcaption>Toshiba's secondary logo used from 1969 to 1984, used in tandem with the umbrella logo above<sup id="cite_ref-toshiba.co.jp_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-toshiba.co.jp-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Toshiba_logo.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Toshiba_logo.svg/220px-Toshiba_logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="34" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Toshiba_logo.svg/330px-Toshiba_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Toshiba_logo.svg/440px-Toshiba_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="122" /></a><figcaption>Toshiba logo, used since 1984<sup id="cite_ref-toshiba.co.jp_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-toshiba.co.jp-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1987, Tocibai Machine, a subsidiary of Toshiba, was accused of illegally selling CNC <a href="/wiki/Milling_machine" class="mw-redirect" title="Milling machine">milling machines</a> used to produce very quiet <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a> propellers to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in violation of the <a href="/wiki/CoCom" class="mw-redirect" title="CoCom">CoCom</a> agreement, an international <a href="/wiki/Embargo" class="mw-redirect" title="Embargo">embargo</a> on certain countries to <a href="/wiki/COMECON" class="mw-redirect" title="COMECON">COMECON</a> countries. The <a href="/wiki/Toshiba-Kongsberg_scandal" class="mw-redirect" title="Toshiba-Kongsberg scandal">Toshiba-Kongsberg scandal</a> involved a subsidiary of Toshiba and the Norwegian company <a href="/wiki/Kongsberg_Defence_%26_Aerospace" title="Kongsberg Defence &amp; Aerospace">Kongsberg Vaapenfabrikk</a>. The incident strained relations between the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, and resulted in the arrest and prosecution of two senior executives, as well as the imposition of <a href="/wiki/Economic_sanctions" title="Economic sanctions">sanctions</a> on the company by both countries.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Senator <a href="/wiki/John_Heinz" title="John Heinz">John Heinz</a> of Pennsylvania said "What Toshiba and Kongsberg did was ransom the security of the United States for $517 million."
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:TOSHIBA_JW-10.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/TOSHIBA_JW-10.JPG/220px-TOSHIBA_JW-10.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/TOSHIBA_JW-10.JPG/330px-TOSHIBA_JW-10.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/TOSHIBA_JW-10.JPG/440px-TOSHIBA_JW-10.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a><figcaption>World-first Japanese <a href="/wiki/Word_processor" title="Word processor">word processor</a> Toshiba JW-10 (1979)</figcaption></figure>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000_to_2010">2000 to 2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2000 to 2010"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2001, Toshiba signed a contract with <a href="/wiki/Orion_Electric" title="Orion Electric">Orion Electric</a>, one of the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Original_equipment_manufacturer" title="Original equipment manufacturer">OEM</a> consumer video electronic makers and suppliers, to manufacture and supply finished consumer TV and video products for Toshiba to meet the increasing demand for the North American market. The contract ended in 2008, ending seven years of OEM production with Orion.
</p><p>In December 2004, Toshiba quietly announced it would discontinue manufacturing traditional in-house <a href="/wiki/Cathode-ray_tube" title="Cathode-ray tube">cathode-ray tube</a> (CRT) televisions. In 2005, Matsushita Toshiba Picture Display Co. Ltd. (a joint venture between <a href="/wiki/Panasonic" title="Panasonic">Panasonic</a> and Toshiba created in 2002<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>) stopped production of CRTs at its factory in Horseheads, New York. A year later, in 2006, it stopped production at its Malaysian factory, following heavy losses.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, Toshiba terminated sales of CRT TVs in Japan<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and production of in-house plasma TVs. To ensure its future competitiveness in the flat-panel digital television and display market, Toshiba has made a considerable investment in a new kind of display technology called <a href="/wiki/Surface-conduction_electron-emitter_display" title="Surface-conduction electron-emitter display">SED</a>. This technology, however, was never sold to the public, as it was not price-competitive with LCDs. Toshiba sold its share in SED Inc. to <a href="/wiki/Canon_Inc." title="Canon Inc.">Canon</a> after Nano-Proprietary, which owns several patents related to SED technology, claimed SED Inc. was not a subsidiary of Canon.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Before <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Toshiba was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Mitsui" title="Mitsui">Mitsui Group</a> <a href="/wiki/Zaibatsu" title="Zaibatsu">zaibatsu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Family_business" title="Family business">family-controlled</a> <a href="/wiki/Vertical_monopoly" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertical monopoly">vertical monopoly</a>). Today Toshiba is a member of the Mitsui <a href="/wiki/Keiretsu" title="Keiretsu">keiretsu</a> (a set of companies with interlocking business relationships and shareholdings), and still has preferential arrangements with Mitsui Bank and the other members of the keiretsu. Membership in a keiretsu has traditionally meant loyalty, both corporate and private, to other members of the keiretsu or allied keiretsu. This loyalty can extend as far as the <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> the employees consume, which in Toshiba's case is <a href="/wiki/Asahi_Breweries" title="Asahi Breweries">Asahi</a>.
</p><p>In July 2005, <a href="/wiki/BNFL" class="mw-redirect" title="BNFL">BNFL</a> confirmed it planned to sell <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Company" title="Westinghouse Electric Company">Westinghouse Electric Company</a>, then estimated to be worth $1.8 billion (£1 billion).<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The bid attracted interest from several companies including Toshiba, <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Heavy_Industries" title="Mitsubishi Heavy Industries">Mitsubishi Heavy Industries</a> and when the <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i> reported on 23 January 2006 that Toshiba had won the bid, it valued the company's offer at $5 billion (£2.8 billion). The sale of Westinghouse by the Government of the United Kingdom surprised many industry experts, who questioned the wisdom of selling one of the world's largest producers of nuclear reactors shortly before the market for nuclear power was expected to grow substantially; <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Republic of China">China</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> were all expected to invest heavily in nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Company" title="Westinghouse Electric Company">Westinghouse</a> for $5.4 billion was completed on 17 October 2006, with Toshiba obtaining a 77 percent share, and partners <a href="/wiki/The_Shaw_Group" title="The Shaw Group">The Shaw Group</a> a 20 percent share and Ishikawajima-Harima Heavy Industries Co. Ltd. a 3 percent share.
</p><p>In late 2007, Toshiba took over from <a href="/wiki/Discover_Card" title="Discover Card">Discover Card</a> as the sponsor of the top-most screen of <a href="/wiki/One_Times_Square" title="One Times Square">One Times Square</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> It displays the iconic 60-second <a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Eve" title="New Year&#39;s Eve">New Year's</a> countdown on its screen, as well as messages, greetings, and advertisements for the company. The sponsor of the New Year's countdown was taken over by <a href="/wiki/Capital_One" title="Capital One">Capital One</a> on 31 December 2018.
</p><p>In January 2009, Toshiba acquired the <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk_drive" title="Hard disk drive">HDD</a> business of <a href="/wiki/Fujitsu" title="Fujitsu">Fujitsu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_to_2014">2010 to 2014</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2010 to 2014"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Toshiba announced on 16 May 2011, that it had agreed to acquire all of the shares of the Swiss-based advanced-power-meter maker <a href="/wiki/Landis%2BGyr" title="Landis+Gyr">Landis+Gyr</a> for $2.3 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In 2010 the company released a series of television models including the WL768, YL863, VL963 designed in collaboration with Danish designer <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Jacob_Jensen" title="Timothy Jacob Jensen">Timothy Jacob Jensen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
In April 2012, Toshiba agreed to acquire <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>'s point-of-sale business for $850 million, making it the world's largest vendor of point-of-sale systems.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2012, Toshiba was accused of fixing the prices of LCD panels in the United States at a high level. While such claims were denied by Toshiba,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> they agreed to settle alongside several other manufacturers for a total of $571 million.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2013, Toshiba completed its acquisition of Vijai Electricals Limited plant at Hyderabad and set up its own base for manufacturing of transmission and distribution products (transformers and switchgears) under the Social Infrastructure Group in India as Toshiba Transmission &amp; Distribution Systems (India) Private Limited.
In January 2014, Toshiba completed its acquisition of <a href="/wiki/OCZ_Storage_Solutions" class="mw-redirect" title="OCZ Storage Solutions">OCZ Storage Solutions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> OCZ Technology stock was halted on 27 November 2013. OCZ then stated they expected to file a petition for bankruptcy and that Toshiba Corporation had expressed interest in purchasing its assets in a bankruptcy proceeding.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> On 2 December 2013, OCZ announced Toshiba had agreed to purchase nearly all of OCZ's assets for $35 million.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The deal was completed on 21 January 2014 when the assets of OCZ Technology Group became a new independently operated subsidiary of Toshiba named OCZ Storage Solutions.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> OCZ Technology Group then changed its name to ZCO Liquidating Corporation;<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> on 18 August 2014, ZCO Liquidating Corporation and its subsidiaries were liquidated.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/OCZ_Storage_Solutions" class="mw-redirect" title="OCZ Storage Solutions">OCZ Storage Solutions</a> was dissolved on 1 April 2016 and absorbed into Toshiba America Electronic Components, Inc.,<sup id="cite_ref-CompanyWebsite2_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CompanyWebsite2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Valich2_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valich2-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> with OCZ becoming a brand of Toshiba.
</p><p>In March 2014, Toshiba sued <a href="/wiki/SK_Hynix" title="SK Hynix">SK Hynix</a>, accusing the company of stealing technology of its NAND flash memory.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In the late same year, the two companies settled with a deal in which SK Hynix pays US$278 million to Toshiba.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Toshiba had sued Hynix in the early 2000s for <a href="/wiki/Patent_infringement" title="Patent infringement">patent infringement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2014, Toshiba and <a href="/wiki/United_Technologies" title="United Technologies">United Technologies</a> agreed a deal to expand their joint venture outside <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2015_accounting_scandal">2015 accounting scandal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2015 accounting scandal"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Toshiba first announced in May 2015 that it was investigating an accounting scandal and it might have to revise its profits for the previous three years.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> On 21 July 2015, CEO Hisao Tanaka announced his resignation amid an accounting scandal that he called "the most damaging event for our brand in the company's 140-year history". Profits had been inflated by $1.2 billion over the previous seven years.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Eight other senior officials also resigned, including the two previous CEOs.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Chairman Masashi Muromachi was appointed acting CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters-20150721_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters-20150721-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Following the scandal, Toshiba Corp. was removed from a stock index showcasing Japan's best companies. That was the second reshuffle of the index, which picks companies with the best operating income, return on equity and market value.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Toshiba announced in early 2015 that they would stop making televisions in its own factories. From 2015 onward, Toshiba televisions will be made by <a href="/wiki/Compal_Electronics" title="Compal Electronics">Compal</a> for the U.S., or by <a href="/wiki/Vestel" title="Vestel">Vestel</a> and other manufacturers for the European market.
</p><p>In September 2015, Toshiba shares fell to their lowest point in two and a half years. The firm said in a statement that its net losses for the quarterly period were 12.3 billion yen ($102m; £66m). The company noted poor performances in its televisions, home appliances and personal computer businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2015, Toshiba sold the image sensor business to <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2015, Muromachi said the episode had wiped about $8 billion off Toshiba's market value. He forecast a record 550 billion yen (about US$4.6 billion) annual loss and warned the company would have to overhaul its TV and computer businesses. Toshiba would not be raising funds for two years, he said. The next week, a company spokesperson announced Toshiba would seek 300 billion yen ($2.5 billion) in 2016, taking the company's indebtedness to more than 1 trillion yen (about $8.3 billion).<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2016, Toshiba's security division unveiled a new bundle of services for schools that use its surveillance equipment. The program, which is intended for both K-12 and higher education, includes education discounts, alerts, and post-warranty support, among other features, on its IP-based security gear.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2016, Toshiba was preparing to start construction on a cutting-edge new semiconductor plant in Japan that would mass-produce chips based on the ultra-dense flash variant. Toshiba expected to spend approximately 360 billion yen, or $3.2 billion, on the project through May 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2016, Toshiba recalled 100,000 faulty laptop lithium-ion batteries, which were made by <a href="/wiki/Panasonic" title="Panasonic">Panasonic</a>, that can overheat, posing burn and fire hazards to consumers, according to the U.S. Consumer Product Safety Commission. Toshiba first announced the recall in January and said it was recalling the batteries in certain Toshiba Notebook computers sold since June 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2016, it was announced that Satoshi Tsunakawa, the former head of Toshiba's medical equipment division, was named CEO. This appointment came after the accounting scandal that occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2016, Toshiba announced the first wireless power receiver <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">IC</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Qi_(standard)" title="Qi (standard)">Qi</a> 1.2.2 specification, developed in association with the <a href="/wiki/Wireless_Power_Consortium" title="Wireless Power Consortium">Wireless Power Consortium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2016, <a href="/wiki/Toshiba_Medical_Systems_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Toshiba Medical Systems Corporation">Toshiba Medical Systems Corporation</a> was acquired by <a href="/wiki/Canon_Inc." title="Canon Inc.">Canon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A Chinese electrical appliance corporation <a href="/wiki/Midea_Group" title="Midea Group">Midea Group</a> bought a controlling 80.1% stake in the Toshiba Home Appliances Group.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2017_US_nuclear_construction_liabilities">2017 US nuclear construction liabilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 2017 US nuclear construction liabilities"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In late December 2016, the management of Toshiba requested an "urgent press briefing" to announce that the newly-found losses in the <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Company" title="Westinghouse Electric Company">Westinghouse</a> subsidiary from <a href="/wiki/Vogtle_Electric_Generating_Plant" title="Vogtle Electric Generating Plant">Vogtle Electric Generating Plant</a> nuclear plant construction would lead to a write-down of several billion dollars, bankrupting Westinghouse and threatening to bankrupt Toshiba. The exact amount of the liabilities was unavailable.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2017, a person with direct knowledge of the matter reported that the company plans on making its memory chip division a separate business, to save Toshiba from bankruptcy.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2017, Toshiba revealed unaudited details of a 390 billion yen ($3.4 billion) corporate wide loss, mainly arising from its majority owned US based <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Company" title="Westinghouse Electric Company">Westinghouse</a> nuclear construction subsidiary which was written down by 712 billion yen ($6.3 billion). On 14 February 2017, Toshiba delayed filing financial results, and chairman Shigenori Shiga, formerly chairman of Westinghouse, resigned.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters-20170214_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters-20170214-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc-20170214a_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20170214a-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc-20170214b_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20170214b-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Construction delays, regulatory changes and cost overruns at Westinghouse-built nuclear facilities <a href="/wiki/Vogtle_Electric_Generating_Plant" title="Vogtle Electric Generating Plant">Vogtle units 3 and 4</a> in Waynesboro, Georgia and <a href="/wiki/Virgil_C._Summer_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Virgil C. Summer Nuclear Generating Station">VC Summer units 2 and 3</a> in South Carolina, were cited as the main causes of the dramatic fall in Toshiba's financial performance and collapse in the share price. Fixed priced construction contracts negotiated by Westinghouse with Georgia Power left Toshiba with uncharted liabilities that resulted in the sale of key Toshiba operating subsidiaries to secure the company's future.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Westinghouse filed for <a href="/wiki/Chapter_11_bankruptcy" class="mw-redirect" title="Chapter 11 bankruptcy">Chapter 11 bankruptcy</a> protection on 29 March 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters-20170330_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters-20170330-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Toshiba was estimated to have 9 billion dollar annual net loss.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 11 April 2017, Toshiba filed unaudited quarterly results. Auditors <a href="/wiki/PricewaterhouseCoopers" class="mw-redirect" title="PricewaterhouseCoopers">PricewaterhouseCoopers</a> had not signed of the accounts because of uncertainties at Westinghouse. Toshiba stated that "substantial doubt about the company's ability to continue as a going concern exists".<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-20170411_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-20170411-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasdaq-20170411_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nasdaq-20170411-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> On 25 April 2017, Toshiba announced its decision to replace its auditor after less than a year. Earlier in April, the company filed twice-delayed business results without an endorsement from auditor PricewaterhouseCoopers (PwC).<sup id="cite_ref-:0_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 20 September 2017, Toshiba's board approved a deal to sell its memory chip business to a group led by <a href="/wiki/Bain_Capital" title="Bain Capital">Bain Capital</a> for US$18 billion, with financial backing by companies such as <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dell_Technologies" title="Dell Technologies">Dell Technologies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hoya_Corporation" title="Hoya Corporation">Hoya Corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingston_Technology" title="Kingston Technology">Kingston Technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seagate_Technology" title="Seagate Technology">Seagate Technology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/SK_Hynix" title="SK Hynix">SK Hynix</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsj-cimiluca_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-cimiluca-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The newly independent company was named <a href="/wiki/Toshiba_Memory_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Toshiba Memory Corporation">Toshiba Memory Corporation</a>, and then renamed <a href="/wiki/Kioxia" title="Kioxia">Kioxia</a>.
</p><p>On 15 November 2017, <a href="/wiki/Hisense" title="Hisense">Hisense</a> reached a deal to acquire 95% of Toshiba Visual Solutions (<a href="/wiki/Television_set" title="Television set">television sets</a>) for US$113.6 million.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Later that month, the company announced that it would pull out of its long-standing sponsorships of the Japanese television programs <i><a href="/wiki/Sazae-san" title="Sazae-san">Sazae-san</a></i>, <i>Nichiyō Gekijo</i>, and the video screens topping out <a href="/wiki/One_Times_Square" title="One Times Square">One Times Square</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. The company cited that the value of these placements were reduced by its exit from consumer-oriented lines of business.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 6 April 2018, Toshiba announced the completion of the sale of Westinghouse's holding company to <a href="/wiki/Brookfield_Business_Partners" title="Brookfield Business Partners">Brookfield Business Partners</a> and some partners for $4.6 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-wnn-20180406_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wnn-20180406-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Present_and_future">Present and future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Present and future"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Lazona_Kawasaki_Toshiba_Building.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Toshiba Science Museum in Kawasaki, Japan"><img alt="Toshiba Science Museum in Kawasaki, Japan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Lazona_Kawasaki_Toshiba_Building.jpg/120px-Lazona_Kawasaki_Toshiba_Building.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="90" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Lazona_Kawasaki_Toshiba_Building.jpg/180px-Lazona_Kawasaki_Toshiba_Building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Lazona_Kawasaki_Toshiba_Building.jpg/240px-Lazona_Kawasaki_Toshiba_Building.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3512" data-file-height="2636" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext"><a href="/wiki/Toshiba_Science_Institute" title="Toshiba Science Institute">Toshiba Science Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kawasaki,_Kanagawa" title="Kawasaki, Kanagawa">Kawasaki</a>, Japan</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Toshiba_Himeji.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Toshiba factory in Taishi, Japan"><img alt="Toshiba factory in Taishi, Japan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Toshiba_Himeji.jpg/120px-Toshiba_Himeji.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="90" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Toshiba_Himeji.jpg/180px-Toshiba_Himeji.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Toshiba_Himeji.jpg/240px-Toshiba_Himeji.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Toshiba factory in <a href="/wiki/Taishi,_Hy%C5%8Dgo" title="Taishi, Hyōgo">Taishi</a>, Japan</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Elevator_Research_Tower_of_Toshiba_Fuchu_Complex.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Elevator Research Tower of Toshiba Fuchu Complex. 			<div class="gallerytext">Elevator Research Tower of Toshiba Fuchu Complex. The largest factory complex in the Toshiba organization</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Toshiba_Rinkan_Hospital.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Toshiba Rinkan Hospital, Kanagawa, Japan"><img alt="Toshiba Rinkan Hospital, Kanagawa, Japan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Toshiba_Rinkan_Hospital.jpg/120px-Toshiba_Rinkan_Hospital.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="90" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Toshiba_Rinkan_Hospital.jpg/180px-Toshiba_Rinkan_Hospital.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Toshiba_Rinkan_Hospital.jpg/240px-Toshiba_Rinkan_Hospital.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Toshiba Rinkan Hospital, <a href="/wiki/Kanagawa" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanagawa">Kanagawa</a>, Japan</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<p>In June 2018, Toshiba sold 80.1% of its Client Solutions (<a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computers</a>) business unit to <a href="/wiki/Sharp_Corporation" title="Sharp Corporation">Sharp</a> for $36m, with an option allowing Sharp to buy the remaining 19.9% share.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Sharp renamed the business to <a href="/wiki/Dynabook_Inc." title="Dynabook Inc.">Dynabook</a>, a brand name Toshiba had used in Japan, and started releasing products under that name. On  30 June 2020, Sharp exercised its option to acquire the remaining 19.9% percent of Dynabook shares from Toshiba.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2019, Toshiba announced that it would put non-Japanese investors on its board for the first time in nearly 80 years.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In November, the company transferred its logistics service business to SBS Group.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2020, Toshiba unveiled its plan to launch quantum cryptography services by September the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-quantumToshiba_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quantumToshiba-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It also announced a number of other technologies waiting for commercialization, including an affordable solid-state <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">Lidar</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Silicon_photomultiplier" title="Silicon photomultiplier">silicon photomultiplier</a>, high-capacity <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fuel_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen fuel cells">hydrogen fuel cells</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> and a proprietary <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">computer algorithm</a> named <i>Simulated Bifurcation Algorithm</i> that mimics <a href="/wiki/Quantum_computing" title="Quantum computing">quantum computing</a>, of which it plans to sell access to other parties such as financial institutions, social networking services, etc. The company claims the algorithm running on a desktop PC at room temperature environment is capable of surpassing the performance of similar algorithms running on existing <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer" title="Supercomputer">supercomputers</a>, even that of laser-based quantum computer when a specialized setting is given.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> It has been added to quantum computing services offered by major cloud platforms including <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Azure" title="Microsoft Azure">Microsoft Azure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2020, Toshiba made a decision to pull out of the system <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit#LSI" title="Integrated circuit">LSI</a> business citing mounted losses while reportedly mulling on the sale of its semiconductor fabs as well.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In April 2021, <a href="/wiki/CVC_Capital_Partners" title="CVC Capital Partners">CVC Capital Partners</a> made a takeover offer.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 12 November 2021, Toshiba announced that it would split into three separate companies. Two of the companies will respectively focus on infrastructure and electronic devices; the third, which will retain the Toshiba name, would manage the 40.6% stake in <a href="/wiki/Kioxia" title="Kioxia">Kioxia</a> and all other remaining assets. The company expects to complete the plan by March 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-Breakup_-_WSJ_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Breakup_-_WSJ-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Toshiba announced in February 2022 that it plans to split into two companies instead after the original proposal proved unpopular with shareholders.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2023 Toshiba Corporation was bought for US$14 billion by a consortium of 20 companies, which was led by Japan Industrial Partners and includes <a href="/wiki/Orix" title="Orix">Orix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chubu_Electric_Power" title="Chubu Electric Power">Chubu Electric Power</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rohm" title="Rohm">Rohm</a>. This move brought the company back to Japan after it had been run by overseas <a href="/wiki/Activist_investor" class="mw-redirect" title="Activist investor">activist investors</a>. The company was delisted on 20 December 2023.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Operations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Toshiba_subsidiaries" title="List of Toshiba subsidiaries">List of Toshiba subsidiaries</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:TOSHIBA_research_and_development_center_Komukaitoshiba.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="The Toshiba research and development facility in Kawasaki, Kanagawa, Japan"><img alt="The Toshiba research and development facility in Kawasaki, Kanagawa, Japan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/TOSHIBA_research_and_development_center_Komukaitoshiba.jpg/120px-TOSHIBA_research_and_development_center_Komukaitoshiba.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="80" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/TOSHIBA_research_and_development_center_Komukaitoshiba.jpg/180px-TOSHIBA_research_and_development_center_Komukaitoshiba.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/TOSHIBA_research_and_development_center_Komukaitoshiba.jpg/240px-TOSHIBA_research_and_development_center_Komukaitoshiba.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a></span></div>

		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:TEG_Gebaeude.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Toshiba Europe offices in Neuss, Germany"><img alt="Toshiba Europe offices in Neuss, Germany" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/TEG_Gebaeude.jpg/120px-TEG_Gebaeude.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="83" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/TEG_Gebaeude.jpg/180px-TEG_Gebaeude.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/TEG_Gebaeude.jpg/240px-TEG_Gebaeude.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="441" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Toshiba Europe offices in <a href="/wiki/Neuss" title="Neuss">Neuss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></div>
		</li>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:ToshibaCanada2.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Toshiba Canada offices"><img alt="Toshiba Canada offices" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/ToshibaCanada2.jpg/120px-ToshibaCanada2.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="80" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/ToshibaCanada2.jpg/180px-ToshibaCanada2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/ToshibaCanada2.jpg/240px-ToshibaCanada2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2138" data-file-height="1426" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Toshiba Canada offices</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<p>As of 2012, Toshiba had 39 R&amp;D facilities worldwide, which employed around 4,180 people,<sup id="cite_ref-annrepop2012_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrepop2012-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> and was organized into four main business groupings: the Digital Products Group, the Electronic Devices Group, the Home Appliances Group and the Social Infrastructure Group.<sup id="cite_ref-annrepop2012_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrepop2012-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In the year ended 31 March 2012, Toshiba had total revenues of <span style="white-space: nowrap">¥6,100.3 billion</span>, of which 25.2 percent was generated by the Digital Products Group, 24.5 percent by the Electronic Devices Group, 8.7 percent by the Home Appliances Group, 36.6 percent by the Social Infrastructure Group and 5 percent by other activities. In the same year, 45 percent of Toshiba's sales were generated in Japan and 55 percent in the rest of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-annrepop2012_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrepop2012-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Toshiba invested a total of <span style="white-space: nowrap">¥319.9 billion</span> in R&amp;D in the year ended 31 March 2012, equivalent to 5.2 percent of sales.<sup id="cite_ref-annrepop2012_113-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrepop2012-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Toshiba registered a total of 2,483 patents in the United States in 2011, the fifth-largest number of any company (after <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samsung_Electronics" title="Samsung Electronics">Samsung Electronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canon_Inc." title="Canon Inc.">Canon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panasonic" title="Panasonic">Panasonic</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-annrepop2012_113-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annrepop2012-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Toshiba had around 141,256 employees as of 31 March 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-corporatedata_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-corporatedata-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Products.2C_services.2C_and_standards"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Products,_services,_and_standards">Products, services, and standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Products, services, and standards"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Toshiba has had a range of products and services, including air conditioners,<sup id="cite_ref-tceh_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tceh-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> consumer electronics (including televisions and DVD and Blu-ray players),<sup id="cite_ref-dps_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dps-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> control systems (including air-traffic control systems, railway systems, security systems and traffic control systems),<sup id="cite_ref-sisc_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sisc-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> electronic point of sale equipment,<sup id="cite_ref-tec_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tec-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> elevators and escalators,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> home appliances (including refrigerators and washing machines),<sup id="cite_ref-tceh_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tceh-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> IT services,<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> lighting,<sup id="cite_ref-tceh_115-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tceh-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> materials and electronic components,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> medical equipment (including CT and MRI scanners, ultrasound equipment and X-ray equipment),<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> office equipment,<sup id="cite_ref-tec_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tec-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> business telecommunication equipment<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> personal computers,<sup id="cite_ref-dps_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dps-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> semiconductors,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> power systems (including electricity turbines, fuel cells and nuclear reactors)<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> power transmission and distribution systems,<sup id="cite_ref-sisc_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sisc-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> and TFT displays.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:JGSDF_Type_93_Surface-to-air_missile(04-4187)_right_front_view_at_Camp_Shinodayama_April_16,_2017_03.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Toshiba Type 93 surface-to-air missile"><img alt="Toshiba Type 93 surface-to-air missile" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/JGSDF_Type_93_Surface-to-air_missile%2804-4187%29_right_front_view_at_Camp_Shinodayama_April_16%2C_2017_03.jpg/120px-JGSDF_Type_93_Surface-to-air_missile%2804-4187%29_right_front_view_at_Camp_Shinodayama_April_16%2C_2017_03.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="68" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/JGSDF_Type_93_Surface-to-air_missile%2804-4187%29_right_front_view_at_Camp_Shinodayama_April_16%2C_2017_03.jpg/180px-JGSDF_Type_93_Surface-to-air_missile%2804-4187%29_right_front_view_at_Camp_Shinodayama_April_16%2C_2017_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/JGSDF_Type_93_Surface-to-air_missile%2804-4187%29_right_front_view_at_Camp_Shinodayama_April_16%2C_2017_03.jpg/240px-JGSDF_Type_93_Surface-to-air_missile%2804-4187%29_right_front_view_at_Camp_Shinodayama_April_16%2C_2017_03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="3376" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Toshiba <a href="/wiki/Type_93_surface-to-air_missile" title="Type 93 surface-to-air missile">Type 93 surface-to-air missile</a></div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Type_81_SAM_-_launcher.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Toshiba Tan-SAM Type 81 SAM 6 x 6 launcher"><img alt="Toshiba Tan-SAM Type 81 SAM 6 x 6 launcher" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Type_81_SAM_-_launcher.jpg/120px-Type_81_SAM_-_launcher.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="74" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Type_81_SAM_-_launcher.jpg/180px-Type_81_SAM_-_launcher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Type_81_SAM_-_launcher.jpg/240px-Type_81_SAM_-_launcher.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3843" data-file-height="2368" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Toshiba <a href="/wiki/Type_81_(missile)" title="Type 81 (missile)">Tan-SAM Type 81 SAM 6 x 6 launcher</a></div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:ABWR_Toshiba_1.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Model of the nuclear power plant from Toshiba with Advanced boiling water reactor"><img alt="Model of the nuclear power plant from Toshiba with Advanced boiling water reactor" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/ABWR_Toshiba_1.jpg/120px-ABWR_Toshiba_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="90" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/ABWR_Toshiba_1.jpg/180px-ABWR_Toshiba_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/ABWR_Toshiba_1.jpg/240px-ABWR_Toshiba_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a></span></div>
