</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="HD_DVD">HD DVD</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: HD DVD"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HD_DVD" title="HD DVD">HD DVD</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062633282">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .content .thumb>div:not(.thumbinner){display:flex;justify-content:center;flex-wrap:wrap;align-content:flex-start;flex-direction:column}}body.skin-vector .mw-parser-output div.thumb>div:not(.thumbinner){font-size:94%;text-align:center;overflow:hidden;min-width:100px}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output div.thumb>div:not(.thumbinner){margin:0 auto;max-width:100%!important}</style> 
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="display:table;">
<span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:HD-DVD.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="HD DVD logo"><img alt="HD DVD logo" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HD-DVD.svg/220px-HD-DVD.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="56" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HD-DVD.svg/330px-HD-DVD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HD-DVD.svg/440px-HD-DVD.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="249" data-file-height="63" /></a></span><div class="thumbcaption" style="display:table-caption;caption-side:bottom;box-sizing:border-box;">HD DVD logo </div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Toshiba had played a critical role in the development and proliferation of <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVD</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> On 19 February 2008, Toshiba announced that it would be discontinuing its HD DVD storage format, the successor of DVD, following defeat in <a href="/wiki/High-definition_optical_disc_format_war" title="High-definition optical disc format war">a format war</a> against <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The HD DVD format had failed after most of the major US film studios backed the Blu-ray format, which was developed by <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a>, Panasonic, <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">Philips</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Corporation" title="Pioneer Corporation">Pioneer Corporation</a>. Conceding the abandonment of HD DVD, Toshiba's president, <a href="/wiki/Atsutoshi_Nishida" title="Atsutoshi Nishida">Atsutoshi Nishida</a> said "We concluded that a swift decision would be best [and] if we had continued, that would have created problems for consumers, and we simply had no chance to win".<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Toshiba continued to supply retailers with machines until the end of March 2008, and continued to provide technical support to the estimated one million people worldwide who owned HD DVD players and recorders. Toshiba announced a new line of stand-alone Blu-ray players as well as drives for PCs and laptops, and subsequently joined the BDA, the industry body which oversees the development of the Blu-ray format.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="REGZA">REGZA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: REGZA"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062633282"> 
<div class="thumb tright">
<div style="display:table;">
<span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:REGZA_logo.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="REGZA wordmark"><img alt="REGZA wordmark" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/REGZA_logo.svg/220px-REGZA_logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="32" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/REGZA_logo.svg/330px-REGZA_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/REGZA_logo.svg/440px-REGZA_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="535" data-file-height="77" /></a></span><div class="thumbcaption" style="display:table-caption;caption-side:bottom;box-sizing:border-box;">REGZA wordmark </div>
</div>
</div>
<p>REGZA (<b>R</b>eal <b>E</b>xpression <b>G</b>uaranteed by Ama<b>z</b>ing <b>A</b>rchitecture) is a unified television brand owned and manufactured by Toshiba. In 2010 REGZA name disappeared from the North American market, and from March 2015<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> new TVs carrying the Toshiba name are designed and produced by <a href="/wiki/Compal_Electronics" title="Compal Electronics">Compal Electronics</a>, a Taiwanese company, to which Toshiba has licensed its name. REGZA is also used in <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a>-based smartphones that were developed by Fujitsu Toshiba Mobile Communications.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="3D_television">3D television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 3D television"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In October 2010, Toshiba unveiled the Toshiba Regza GL1 21" LED-backlit LCD TV glasses-free <a href="/wiki/3D_television" title="3D television">3D prototype</a> at <a href="/wiki/CEATEC" title="CEATEC">CEATEC</a> 2010. This system supports 3D capability without glasses (utilizing an integral imaging system of 9 parallax images with a vertical lenticular sheet). The retail product was released in December 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="4K_Ultra_HD_televisions">4K Ultra HD televisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 4K Ultra HD televisions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/4K_resolution" title="4K resolution">4K Ultra HD</a> (3840×2160p) televisions provides four times the resolution of 1080p Full HD televisions. Toshiba's 4K HD LED televisions are powered by a CEVO 4K Quad + dual-core processor.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laptops">Laptops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Laptops"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dynabook_Inc." title="Dynabook Inc.">Dynabook Inc.</a></div>
<p>In 1985, Toshiba released the <a href="/wiki/Toshiba_T1100" title="Toshiba T1100">T1100</a>, the world's first commercially accepted <a href="/wiki/Laptop" title="Laptop">laptop PC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Toshiba created laptops under several product lines including <a href="/wiki/Dynabook_Satellite" title="Dynabook Satellite">Satellite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dynabook_Port%C3%A9g%C3%A9" title="Dynabook Portégé">Portégé</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toshiba_Libretto" title="Toshiba Libretto">Libretto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qosmio" title="Qosmio">Qosmio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dynabook_Tecra" title="Dynabook Tecra">Tecra</a>.
</p><p>In October 2014, Toshiba released the <a href="/wiki/Chromebook" title="Chromebook">Chromebook</a> 2, a new version with a thinner profile and a much-improved display. The Chromebook runs exclusively on <a href="/wiki/ChromeOS" title="ChromeOS">ChromeOS</a> and gives users free Google Drive storage and access to a collection of apps and extensions at the Chrome Web Store.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Toshiba initialized process of divestment of the personal computer and laptop business, Toshiba Client Solutions, in 2018 with sale of 80.1% of shares to <a href="/wiki/Sharp_Corporation" title="Sharp Corporation">Sharp Corporation</a>. Eventually Toshiba fully exited from the personal computing market in June 2020, transferring the remaining 19.9% shares in Toshiba Client Solutions (since being renamed to <a href="/wiki/Dynabook_Inc." title="Dynabook Inc.">Dynabook Inc.</a>) to Sharp.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Toshiba's divested personal computing business adopted the Dynabook name after a <a href="/wiki/Dynabook" title="Dynabook">computer concept targeted for children</a> and after one of its product lines.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flash_memory">Flash memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Flash memory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1980s, a Toshiba team led by <a href="/wiki/Fujio_Masuoka" title="Fujio Masuoka">Fujio Masuoka</a> invented <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">flash memory</a>, both NOR and NAND types. In March 2015, Toshiba announced the development of the first 48-layer, three-dimensional flash memory. The new flash memory is based on a vertical stacking technology that Toshiba calls BiCS (Bit Cost Scaling), stores two bits of data per transistor, and can store 128Gbits (16GB) per chip. This allowed flash memory to keep scaling up the capacity as <a href="/wiki/Moore%27s_law" title="Moore&#39;s law">Moore's Law</a> was considered to be obsolete.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Toshiba's memory division was spun off as <a href="/wiki/Toshiba_Memory_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Toshiba Memory Corporation">Toshiba Memory Corporation</a>, now <a href="/wiki/Kioxia" title="Kioxia">Kioxia</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_record">Environmental record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Environmental record"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Toshiba has been judged as making "low" efforts to lessen its impact on the environment. In November 2012, they came second from the bottom in <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a>'s 18th edition of the Guide to Greener Electronics that ranks electronics companies according to their policies on products, energy, and sustainable operations.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenpeace_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenpeace-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Toshiba received 2.3 of a possible 10 points, with the top company (<a href="/wiki/WIPRO" class="mw-redirect" title="WIPRO">WIPRO</a>) receiving 7.1 points. "Zero" scores were received in the categories "Clean energy policy advocacy", "Use of recycled plastics in products" and "Policy and practice on <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_sourcing" title="Sustainable sourcing">sustainable sourcing</a> of fibres for paper".
</p><p>In 2010, Toshiba reported that all of its new LCD TVs comply with the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Star" title="Energy Star">Energy Star</a> standards and 34 models exceed the requirements by 30% or more.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Toshiba also partnered with China's Tsinghua University in 2008 in order to form a research facility to focus on energy conservation and the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-japancorp.net_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-japancorp.net-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The new Toshiba Energy and Environment Research Center is located in Beijing where forty students from the university will work to research electric power equipment and new technologies that will help stop the global warming process.<sup id="cite_ref-japancorp.net_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-japancorp.net-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Through this partnership, Toshiba hopes to develop products that will better protect the environment and save China.<sup id="cite_ref-japancorp.net_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-japancorp.net-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> This contract between Tsinghua University and Toshiba originally began in October 2007 when they signed an agreement on joint energy and environment research.<sup id="cite_ref-japancorp.net_141-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-japancorp.net-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The projects that they conduct work to reduce car pollution and to create power systems that don't negatively affect the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-japancorp.net_141-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-japancorp.net-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 28 December 1970 Toshiba began the construction of unit 3 of the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant</a><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> which was damaged in the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_I_nuclear_accidents" class="mw-redirect" title="Fukushima I nuclear accidents">Fukushima I nuclear accidents</a> on 14 March 2011. In April 2011, CEO Norio Sasaki declared nuclear energy would "remain as a strong option" even after the Fukushima I nuclear accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In late 2013, Toshiba (Japan) entered the solar power business in Germany, installing PV systems on apartment buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Toshiba&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: See also"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
